        <link rel="schema.DC" href="http://purl.org/DC/elements/1.0/"><meta name="dc.Title" content="Ultralong Sb2Se3 Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Wen  Luo"><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Armand  Calas"><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Chunjuan  Tang"><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Feng  Li"><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Liang  Zhou"><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Liqiang  Mai"><meta name="dc.Subject" content="antimony triselenide (Sb2Se3); nanowire; free-standing membrane; lithium ion battery; sodium ion battery; anode"><meta name="dc.Description" content="Metal chalcogenides have emerged as promising anode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs).         <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.6b11544"><meta property="og:title" content="Ultralong Sb2Se3 Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/acsami.6b11544.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Metal chalcogenides have emerged as promising anode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a free-standing membrane based on ultralong Sb2Se3 nanowires has been successfully fabricated via a facile hydrothermal synthesis combined with a subsequent vacuum filtration treatment. The as-achieved free-standing membrane constructed by pure Sb2Se3 nanowires exhibits good flexibility and integrity. Meanwhile, we investigate the lithium and sodium storage behavior of the Sb2Se3 nanowire-based free-standing membrane. When applied as the anode for LIBs, it delivers a reversible capacity of 614 mA h g–1 at 100 mA g–1, maintaining 584 mA h g–1 after 50 cycles. When applied as the anode for SIBs, it delivers a reversible capacity of 360 mA h g–1 at 100 mA g–1, retaining 289 mA h g–1 after 50 cycles. Such difference in electrochemical performance can be attributed to the more complex sodiation process relative to the corresponding lithiation process. This work may provide insight on developing Sb2Se3-based anode materials for high-performance LIBs or SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/acsami.6b11544.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e7d11eb7775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e7d11eb7775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '368e4b4f-23c0-470c-b6ff-1b0165821a4b'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e7d11eb7775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultralong Sb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;Se&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanowire-Based Free-Standing Membrane Anode for Lithium/Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b11544", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

E-mail: <a href="mailto:mlq518@whut.edu.cn">mlq518@whut.edu.cn</a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" name="pub-doi" value="10.1021/acsami.6b11544"><a href="/doi/epdf/10.1021/acsami.6b11544?ref=article_openPDF" title="PDF" target="_blank" class="article__btn__secondary" data-id="article_header_OpenPDF"><i class="icon-open-pdf"></i><span>Open PDF</span></a><a href="#_i6" title="Supporting Information" class="article__btn__secondary internalNav" data-id="article_header_SupportingInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Information (1)</span></a><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;type=refOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3Fid%3Ddoi%3A10.1021%252Facsami.6b11544%26sid%3Dliteratum%253Aachs%26pmid%3D27959503%26genre%3Darticle%26aulast%3DLuo%26date%3D2016%26atitle%3DUltralong%2BSb2Se3%2BNanowire-Based%2BFree-Standing%2BMembrane%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BLithium%252FSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26jtitle%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26title%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26volume%3D8%26issue%3D51%26spage%3D35219%26epage%3D35226%26issn%3D1944-8244" title="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON" class="issue-item_open-url sfxLink"><img src="/userimages/9157ccea-6c85-4e5f-ac2a-6a9fd9422c17/sfxbutton-180e067c810" alt="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" name="journalNameForjhpLink" value="ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces"><div class="article_header-journal"><h2>ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</h2></div><div class="article_header-cite-this" tabindex="0"><input type="hidden" name="citation-copy-value" value="ACS Appl. class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D35219%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWen%2BLuo%252C%2BArmand%2BCalas%252C%2BChunjuan%2BTang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D51%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b11544%26title%3DUltralong%2BSb2Se3%2BNanowire-Based%2BFree-Standing%2BMembrane%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BLithium%252FSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D35226%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2016" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/medium/am-2016-11544m_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Metal chalcogenides have emerged as promising anode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a free-standing membrane based on ultralong Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires has been successfully fabricated via a facile hydrothermal synthesis combined with a subsequent vacuum filtration treatment. The as-achieved free-standing membrane constructed by pure Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires exhibits good flexibility and integrity. Meanwhile, we investigate the lithium and sodium storage behavior of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowire-based free-standing membrane. When applied as the anode for LIBs, it delivers a reversible capacity of 614 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, maintaining 584 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles. When applied as the anode for SIBs, it delivers a reversible capacity of 360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, retaining 289 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles. Such difference in electrochemical performance can be attributed to the more complex sodiation process relative to the corresponding lithiation process. This work may provide insight on developing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-based anode materials for high-performance LIBs or SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D35219%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWen%2BLuo%252C%2BArmand%2BCalas%252C%2BChunjuan%2BTang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D51%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b11544%26title%3DUltralong%2BSb2Se3%2BNanowire-Based%2BFree-Standing%2BMembrane%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BLithium%252FSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D35226%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291691&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Membranes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Membranes" class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony+Triselenide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony%20triselenide%20%28Sb2Se3%29">antimony triselenide (Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>)</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanowire&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanowire">nanowire</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Free-standing+Membrane&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_free-standing%20membrane">free-standing membrane</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lithium%20ion%20battery">lithium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20battery">sodium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The ever-dwindling fossil fuel levels and the serious environmental pollution caused by fossil fuel combustion have triggered urgent demands for high-efficiency renewable energy storage and conversion devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> Lithium ion batteries (LIBs), as prominent energy storage devices, are attracting great attention for portable electronics and electric vehicles (EVs), as well as large-scale grid application. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4, 5)</a> Despite this, great concerns about the scarcity of lithium (Li) resources and the rising price of Li have accelerated the search for a viable and reliable alternative to LIBs. Recently, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have captured intense research interests due to the merits of abundant, low-cost sodium (Na) resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6-9)</a> However, even though sodium shares many similar characteristics with lithium, the sodiation processes differentiate substantially from the lithiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 and 10">(8, 10)</a> Because of the larger ionic radius of Na ion, compared to that of Li ion, sluggish electrochemical reaction kinetics is often associated with the SIBs. This implies that some host materials and mature strategies suitable for LIBs can hardly be directly applied to SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11, 12)</a> For instance, graphite, a well-known commercial anode for LIBs, provides only a negligible Na storage capacity of 30–50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Si, which has an ultrahigh Li storage capacity of 4200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14, 15)</a> cannot store Na ions reversibly at ambient environment. Therefore, the development of high-performance anode materials for energy storage devices and the revelation of their intrinsic charge storage mechanism remain.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, antimony (Sb) has been intensively investigated as anode material for LIBs and SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16, 17)</a> Through an alloying/dealloying process, Sb can deliver a high specific capacity up to 660 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Up to now, the performances of Sb have been significantly improved through structure design, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18, 19)</a> carbon modification, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 20 to 27">(20-27)</a> intermetallic alloying strategy, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 28 to 31">(28-31)</a> and so on. Recently, Sb-based chalcogenides, namely, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, have captured increasing research interests due to their higher theoretical capacity relative to that of Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32-34)</a> Taking Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> as an example, it first undergoes a conversion reaction with Li/Na, followed by an alloying process. Thus, per formula Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> can accommodate 12 Li/Na ions during the first discharge, contributing to a high capacity of 946 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> With regard to Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, Jiang et al. found that the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>@C prepared via high-energy mechanical ball milling of Sb, Se, and carbon black showed a reversible capacity of 650 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The high capacity and enhanced electrochemical utilization were attributed to the introduction of Se. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">One-dimensional (1D) nanostructures have been widely utilized in various electrochemical storage devices and achieved improved performance due to their strain accommodation ability and fast charge transport. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36, 37)</a> In addition, nanowires with micrometer length and rich functional groups can be assembled into free-standing and flexible membrane electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> For example, Gao et al. reported the fabrication of a free-standing 3D Ni@NiO nanowire-based membrane by a facile filtration process, which manifests a high capacitance retention of 89% (34.2 F/cm<sup>3</sup>) after 3000 cycles at a high discharge current density of 0.512 A/cm<sup>3</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Yu et al. fabricated a free-standing Pt nanowire-based membrane with good electrocatalytic activity and remarkably high stability due to its unique network structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> More importantly, an additive-free electrode can eliminate the side effects from nonconductive polymeric binder or conductive carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41, 42)</a> As a V–VI class material, Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> tends to grow into 1D nanostructures, such as nanowires, nanorods, and nanoribbons. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 43 to 46">(43-46)</a> To the knowledge of our best, there is no report of an additive-free free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowire-based membrane electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, a free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane has been fabricated via a facile hydrothermal synthesis combined with a subsequent vacuum filtration process. We then investigated the lithium and sodium storage performances of the resultant Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane. The free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane exhibits good cycling stability and rate performance. Such a free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowire-based membrane electrode provides a well-defined model for investigating the lithium and sodium storage mechanism. It is expected that this work will arouse and broaden the research interest in Sb chalcogenide-based materials for high-performance LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Fabrication and Characterization of Ultralong Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> Nanowire-Based Membrane</h3><div class="NLM_p">The Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires were prepared according to a previously reported method with slight modifications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The synthesis was achieved by adding hydrazine hydrate (N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O) into a mixture of antimony acetate (Sb(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>) and sodium selenite (Na<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>3</sub>), which was followed by a hydrothermal treatment. The detailed experimental parameters can be found in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. The X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires is presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. All of the peaks can be well indexed to the pure Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> phase (JCPDS: 00-015-0861) with orthorhombic crystal structure and <i>Pbnm</i> space group. The sharp diffraction peaks demonstrate good crystallinity of the as-obtained Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires. The structure of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires is further studied by Raman spectroscopy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Three intense peaks at 192, 255, and 452 cm<sup>–1</sup> and two weak peaks at 356 and 373 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be detected. The vibration at 192 cm<sup>–1</sup> could be ascribed to the Sb–Se–Sb bending modes, which was consistent with a previous study. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> It should be noted that the intense peak at 255 cm<sup>–1</sup> is indexed to Sb–Se stretching vibration with long Sb–Se distance, whereas the bands at 356, 373, and 452 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to the Sb–Se stretching vibration with short Sb–Se distance. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires is 6.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, calculated from the nitrogen sorption results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a> shows the typical electron microscopy (SEM) image of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires. The as-synthesized nanowires are featured with an average diameter of 90 nm and a length up to hundreds of micrometers. Elemental mapping results reveal that both Sb and Se are uniformly distributed throughout the nanowires (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b,c</a>). The energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3d</a>) demonstrates that the atomic ratio of Sb and Se is close to 2:3, which confirms the composition of the product.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/medium/am-2016-11544m_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD pattern of ultralong Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires (a); digital photographs of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane (front view (b) and side view (c)); front view SEM images (d, e) and cross-section view SEM image (f) of the achieved membrane; TEM images (g, h) and HRTEM image (i) of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane was obtained via a facile vacuum filtration process. Panels b and c of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> show typical digital photographs of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane. The plain and smooth surface of the membrane indicates the good integrity of the assembled membrane (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The membrane can be bent easily without breaking (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), which demonstrates its good flexibility. Front view SEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d,e) indicate that the as-fabricated membrane is made up of uniform nanowires with an average diameter of 90 nm and a length in hundreds of micrometers. A cross-section view SEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f) shows that the thickness of the as-fabricated membrane is around 25 μm. TEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g,h) indicate that the nanowires are in uniform shape of hundreds of micrometers in length, agreeing well with the SEM observation. The HRTEM image manifests the lattice spacing of about 0.29 nm, which can be assigned to the (301) planes of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>. The above results demonstrate that a stable free-standing membrane based on ultralong Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires has been successfully fabricated.</div><div class="NLM_p">As mentioned in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec1" aria-label="Introduction">Introduction</a>, ultralong nanowires are vital advantages for the fabrication of free-standing membrane electrodes. Controlled experiments were conducted to study the effects of reactant concentration on nanowire morphology. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, with the increase of concentration of N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O from 0.24 mM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a,b</a>, denoted sample C1) to 0.48 mM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c,d</a>, denoted sample C2), the nanorod structure does not change significantly except for the decrease in diameter. As the concentration increases to 0.96 mM, nanowires with diameters of ∼100 nm and lengths of tens of micrometers are achieved (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4e,f</a>, denoted sample C3). When the concentration of N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O further increases to 1.92 mM, ultralong nanowires with diameters of ∼90 nm and lengths of hundreds of micrometers are obtained (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d,e, optimized sample). For comparison, a membrane composed of much shorter nanowires (sample C1) was also fabricated through the same process. From the digital photograph (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), it can be clearly found that the surface of the membrane is much rougher. Besides, the membrane is fragile and nonflexible, which indicates the poor film-forming ability of short nanowires.</div><div class="NLM_p">To better illustrate the formation process of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires, the products obtained at different reaction times were also collected. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the products collected after 6 h of incubation consist of nanowires along with a small fraction of micrometer-size residues. After a hydrothermal treatment of 12 h (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), bundled nanowires are obtained. After 24 h of reaction, long and stable nanowires are achieved (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). When the hydrothermal reaction time is prolonged to 36 h, ultralong and uniform Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires are obtained (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). In general, prolonging the hydrothermal reaction time gives rise to more uniform and longer nanowires.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/medium/am-2016-11544m_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> obtained after hydrothermal treatment of 6 h (a), 12 h (b), 24 h (c), and 36 h (d).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the aforementioned analysis, the possible formation process of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires is depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. When the reducing agent is added into the mixture solution, the Sb–Se complexes are immediately formed. During the hydrothermal treatment, Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nucleates and grows into seeds in the solution. The seeds then grow anisotropically and form 1D nanostructures. With prolonged reaction under high-temperature and high-pressure conditions, the 1D nanostructure eventually splits into orientated Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/medium/am-2016-11544m_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Schematic illustration of the formation process of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Lithium/Sodium Storage Behaviors of Ultralong Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> Nanowire-Based Membrane</h3><div class="NLM_p">The lithium storage behaviors of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane were evaluated by assembly of CR2016 coin cells with lithium metal as the counter and reference electrodes. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a depicts the cyclic voltammogram (CV) profiles of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane anode in LIBs. A working window of 0.01–3 V (vs Li/Li<sup>+</sup>) is selected for the LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref45" aria-label="reference 35 and 45">(35, 45)</a> The CV profiles are collected at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The reactions between Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and lithium are illustrated as follows: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/am-2016-11544m_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/am-2016-11544m_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/medium/am-2016-11544m_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cyclic voltammograms (CV) profiles (a); charge–discharge profiles (b); cycling performance and corresponding Coulombic efficiency (c); and rate performance (d) of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane as a LIB anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, four cathodic peaks appear at 2.01, 1.62, 1.31, and 0.68 V versus Li/Li<sup>+</sup> in the first reduction process. In the subsequent oxidation process, four anodic peaks are observed at 1.08, 1.36, 1.99, and 2.21 V. The cathodic peaks shift to a higher potential from the second cycle onward, whereas the anodic peaks almost remain. Consequently, an electrochemical activation process might occur during the first lithiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> This activation process is caused by the deformation of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires induced by the huge volume change in the first lithiation, along with the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref34" aria-label="reference 16 and 34">(16, 34)</a> In general, the intense pair of reduction/oxidation peaks at 1.31/1.99 V can be attributed to the conversion reaction between Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and Li (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>); another intense pair reduction/oxidation peaks located at 0.68/1.08 V corresponds to the alloying reaction between Sb and Li (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> It is worth noting that the reduction peak located at ∼0.68 V in LIBs is sharp, which is ascribed to a direct change from Sb into Li<sub>3</sub>Sb, and two oxidation peaks of the dealloying process are observed at 1.08 and 1.36 V in anodic scans, corresponding to the multistep reaction, namely, the delithiation process of Li ions from the Li<sub>3</sub>Sb associated with the formation of Sb.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows selected discharge–charge curves of Li–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries. When cycled at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge curves are characterized by two flat plateaus located at 1.35 and 0.72 V. As for the charging process, the profile is featured by a plateau at around 1.10 V and a less obvious plateau at around 2.0 V. The discharge–charge profiles are well consistent with the CV results: the first pair of discharge/charge plateaus at 1.35/2.0 V is related to the conversion reaction of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, and the second pair of discharge/charge plateaus at 0.72/1.10 V is ascribed to the Li–Sb alloying/dealloying process.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c depicts the cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane anode in LIBs. The first discharge and charge capacities are 836 and 630 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The Coulombic efficiency for the first cycle is 75.4%; the irreversible capacity loss resulted from the SEI film formation. The second discharge capacity is 614 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is close to its theoretical capacity (670 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). After 50 cycles, a discharge capacity of 584 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is retained, corresponding to a capacity retention of 95.1% (against the second discharge capacity). In addition, the Coulombic efficiency increases significantly during the first five cycles and stabilizes around 99% in the subsequent processes.</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate performance of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane anode for LIBs was investigated at different current densities, from 100 to 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). The free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane delivers average capacities of 618, 558, 482, 386, and 255 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100, 200, 400, 800, and 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density goes back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity returns to 552 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. As an anode for LIBs, the free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowire-based membrane shows good cycle stability and rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The sodium storage behaviors of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane were also characterized. A working window of 0.01–2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) is selected. The CV profiles for Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a) are notably different from that of the lithiation/delithiation processes. The sodiation of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires also shows an activation behavior, but not as distinct as the lithiation process. The most visible difference is that all of the redox peaks in Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries are about 0.3 V lower than those in Li–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries. The observed lower potential for SIBs results from smaller cohesive energy of inserting alkali into the metal host structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> A similar phenomenon has also been observed in Sn-based anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref49" aria-label="reference 28 and 49">(28, 49)</a> The pair of reduction/oxidation peaks at 1.05/1.51 V is related to the Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> conversion reaction, and the pair of reduction/oxidation peaks at 0.45/0.71 V is ascribed to the Na–Sb alloying process. It should be noted that the conversion process of Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> is detected by two reduction peaks of ∼1.0 and ∼1.21 V, which indicate multistep reactions possibly occur. The reason for the splitting of the oxidation peak at around 1.51 V into two peaks in the second and third cycles remains unclear. Notably, the sodiation/desodiation of Sb shows a different reaction mechanism from the lithiation/delithiation of Sb. Previous studies have shown that Sb transforms to hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb via amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb, whereas Sb directly changes into cubic Li<sub>3</sub>Sb on lithiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The symmetric reduction peak located at ∼0.68 V in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a confirms the direct change from Sb into Li<sub>3</sub>Sb upon lithiation. In comparison, the reduction of Sb to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb results in two peaks, a dominant peak around ∼0.45 V and a broad shoulder at ∼0.68 V, suggesting a two-step alloying process from Sb to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb: Sb → amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb → Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/medium/am-2016-11544m_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Cyclic voltammograms (CV) profiles (a); charge–discharge profiles (b); cycling performance and corresponding Coulombic efficiency (c); and rate performance (d) of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane as a SIB anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/asset/images/large/am-2016-11544m_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b11544&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b exhibits the first, second, and third cycle discharge–charge voltage profiles of the Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries. The first discharge curve is characterized by an inclined plateau at 0.5–1.0 V and a flat plateau at ∼0.5 V. For the second and third discharge processes, two plateaus appear at 1.1 and 0.5 V, consistent with the CV results. In the charge processes, an obvious plateau at 0.6 V and a less distinctive plateau at ∼1.5 V can be observed.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c depicts the cycling performance of the Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries for the first 50 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The first discharge and charge capacities are 606 and 467 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to a Coulombic efficiency of 77.1%. The irreversible capacity loss during the first cycle is due to the formation of SEI film. As in the case of Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries, the Coulombic efficiency gradually increases to near 98% during the following cycles. Capacity decay is observed in the cycling process; the discharge capacity fades to 296 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the rate performance of Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries at different current densities. The discharge capacities at current densities of 100, 200, 400, 800, and 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are 362, 328, 277, 234, and 153 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, on average. When the current density goes back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity returns to 341 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Compared to the lithium storage performance, the sodium storage suffers from low specific capacitance and poor cycling stability. This phenomenon likely originates from the larger radius of the sodium ions relative to those of the lithium ions.</div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement was further conducted to evaluate the resistance and charge transfer properties of the free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane electrode before and after cycling. The depressed high-frequency semicircle represents interface impedance, whereas the low-frequency inclined line is related to the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref49" aria-label="reference 20 and 49">(20, 49)</a> For the Li–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries, the diameter of the high-frequency semicircle increases moderately after 50 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>), suggesting the increase of resistance. In contrast, the slope of the inclined line does not change with cycling, indicating the almost constant ion diffusion rate. With regard to the Na–Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> batteries, the resistance increases more sharply after 50 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>). In addition, the slope of the inclined line decreases significantly after cycling, suggesting the decrease in ion diffusion rate. The sharp increase in charge transfer resistance and decrease in ion diffusivity are responsible for the deterioration in sodium storage performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the structural evolution of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires, ex-situ SEM characterization has been performed. SEM images of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> anode after 50 lithiation/delithiation cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. Compared to the pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires, the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires after cycling retain the one-dimensional morphology with a decreased length. Besides, SEI films covering the full surface of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires can be observed, which might be responsible for the good structural stability of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires against lithiation/delithiation. When used as the anode for SIBs, the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires after 50 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> show a short nanorod morphology with many cracks (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The ex-situ SEM characterization indicates that the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires exhibit good structural stability against the lithiation/delithiation and sodiation/desodiation processes despite the huge volume variation.</div><div class="NLM_p last">For comparison, the electrochemical performances of conventional electrodes composed of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires, carbon black, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) binder were also tested in LIBs and SIBs. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>, the conventional Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> electrode exhibits an initial discharge capacity of ca. 690 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity of 467 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles in LIBs. For the whole cycling process, the conventional Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> electrode delivers a capacity of about 80% of that of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> free-standing membrane anode. A comparison of rate performances is presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>; the conventional Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> electrode exhibits a reasonable response at various current densities. Due to its better conductivity, the conventional Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> electrode delivers higher capacities than the free-standing membrane electrode at high current densities. For instance, the conventional Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> electrode delivers a capacity of 336 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is higher than that of the free-standing membrane anode (255 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). For sodium storage, similar results can be observed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). That is to say, the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowire-based free-standing membrane anode delivers higher specific capacities at low current densities, whereas the conventional Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> electrode has higher capacities at high current densities. Therefore, carbon coating or conductive polymer coating approaches might be required to further enhance the rate capability of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> free-standing membrane anode.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a free-standing Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowire-based membrane has been successfully fabricated via a facile hydrothermal synthesis combined with a subsequent vacuum filtration process. The as-achieved free-standing membrane composed of bare Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires exhibits excellent flexibility and integrity. When applied as the anode for LIBs, it delivers a reversible capacity of 614 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with good cycling stability. When applied as the anode for SIBs, the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-based membrane delivers a lower reversible capacity (360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) with inferior cycling performance. This study demonstrates that Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> has great potential in advanced energy storage devices. It can be anticipated that this work will provide insight on developing high-performance battery anode materials based on conversion–alloying reactions.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b11544" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b11544</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Experimental section including materials synthesis, material characterizations, and electrochemical measurements. Additional Figures S1–S10: Raman spectrum of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires (Figure S1); nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm curve of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowires product (Figure S2); SEM image and corresponding elemental mapping images, EDX spectrum of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> ultralong nanowires (Figure S3); SEM images of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> products obtained at different concentrations of hydrazine hydrate (Figure S4); digital photograph of the membrane fabricated from sample C1 (Figure S5); Nyquist plots of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> nanowire based membrane before cycling and after 50 cycles (Figure S6); SEM images of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane electrode after 50 cycles in LIBs (Figure S7); SEM images of the Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane electrode after 50 cycles in SIBs, at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (Figure S8); comparison of lithium storage performances of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane and conventional electrode with carbon black additive and PVDF binder (Figure S9); comparison of sodium storage performances of Sb<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> membrane and conventional electrode with carbon black additive and PVDF binder (Figure S10) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b11544/suppl_file/am6b11544_si_001.pdf">am6b11544_si_001.pdf (459.62 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b11544" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
